    Abstract

    Heparanase is the only mammalian endo-β-d-glucuronidase involved in a variety of major diseases. The up-regulation of heparanase expression increases tumor size, angiogenesis, and metastasis, representing a validated target in the anti-cancer field. To date, only a few small-molecule inhibitors have been described, but none have gotten through pre-clinical development. Previously, we explored 2-(4-(4-(bromo-methoxybenzamido)benzylamino)phenyl) benzazole derivatives as anti-heparanase agents, proposing this scaffold for development of broadly effective heparanase inhibitors. Herein, we report an extended investigation of new symmetrical 2-aminophenyl-benzazolyl-5-acetate derivatives, proving that symmetrical compounds are more effective than asymmetrical analogues, with the most-potent compound, 7g, being active at nanomolar concentration against heparanase. Molecular docking studies were performed on the best-acting compounds 5c and 7g to rationalize their interaction with the enzyme. Moreover, invasion assay confirmed the anti-metastatic potential of compounds 5c, 7a, and 7g, proving the inhibition of the expression of proangiogenic factors in tumor cells.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Heparan sulfate proteoglycans (HSPGs) are ubiquitous macromolecules associated with the cell surface and extracellular matrix (ECM) of a wide range of cells and have been found to play regulatory roles in many biological functions, including both physiological and pathological processes.(1−3) This class of carbohydrate-modified proteins exerts its multiple functions via several mechanisms combining structural, biochemical, and regulatory aspects. By interacting with other macromolecules such as laminin, fibronectin, and collagens I and IV, HSPGs contribute to the structural integrity, self-assembly, and insolubility of the ECM and basement membrane, thus intimately modulating cell–ECM interactions.(4) Additionally, HSPGs regulate the transfer of information from extracellular space to intracellular kinases and cytoskeletal elements, thus affecting cell signaling, adhesion, and motility.(5)
    The key structural component of cell-surface- and matrix-associated HSPGs is heparan sulfate (HS), whose function is to bind and interact with signaling proteins, growth factors, plasma proteins, immune modulators, and other factors, thereby regulating protein distribution, bioavailability, and action on target cells. Moreover, the HS chains ensure that a wide variety of bioactive molecules bind to the cell surface and ECM, thus regulating morphogenesis, tissue repair, inflammation, vascularization, and cancer metastasis.(6)
    The sole enzyme responsible for the degradation of mammalian HS is heparanase (Hpse), an endo-β-d-glucuronidase that cleaves the HS side chains of HSPGs into segments of 10–20 sugar units(7) by hydrolyzing the β-glycosidic bond at specific intrachain sites with retention of the anomeric configuration, thus producing physiologically active saccharide fragments of 10–20 sugar units and allowing, at the same time, the release of HS-bound macromolecules.(8) By virtue of its activity on HSPGs, Hpse influences a multitude of both normal and disease-related processes.(9) Indeed, under physiological conditions, Hpse is expressed at high levels in placenta and some blood-borne cells such as platelets, neutrophils, mast cells, and lymphocytes, while it is undetectable in other human tissues. On the contrary, the de-regulated expression and function of Hpse has been involved not only in several inflammatory-based diseases (such as chronic inflammation, atherosclerosis, thrombosis, fibrosis, neurite outgrowth, diabetic nephropathy, and bone osteolysis) but also in a variety of human hematological and solid malignancies of both epithelial and mesenchymal origin.(10,11)
    Clinically, Hpse up-regulation has been associated with the growth and aggressiveness of several cancer types, being correlated with an increased tumor size, angiogenesis, metastasis, and poor prognosis.(10) Pioneering and breakthrough studies have highlighted the link between Hpse and cancer. In particular, the significant impairment of tumor growth and progression observed in tumor-bearing mice by Hpse gene expression knock-down or by treatment with Hpse inhibitors has provided the pre-clinical evidence of the potential of Hpse as target in anticancer therapy.(12) Recently, Hpse up-regulation has also been associated with the induction of resistance in response to anticancer therapeutics, an effect attributed, at least in part, to the Hpse-mediated promotion of autophagy.(13) However, chemotherapy itself induces the expression and the release of Hpse by surviving tumor cells, thus contributing to promote an aggressive phenotype.(14)
    Overall, these studies provide a strong rationale to include Hpse inhibitors in combination regimens with chemotherapeutic drugs to overcome chemoresistance. Indeed, compounds able to specifically target Hpse are highly desired to widen therapeutic options in the oncologic context as well as in those clinical indications in which Hpse is pathologically involved. The relevant role of Hpse in sustaining specific pathological conditions has led to the search for molecules able to interfere with its biological activity. However, although, in the last two decades, intensive efforts have been made in developing Hpse inhibitors, and huge progresses have been made in the knowledge of Hpse functions, no drug able to inhibit or modulate Hpse activity and functions has been registered so far.(8,15,16)
    Several Hpse inhibitors have been described, spanning from nucleic acids, proteins, and monoclonal antibodies to polysulfated saccharides. Among them, only four drugs have hitherto reached different phases of clinical trials. They are polysaccharide derivatives of natural origin, such as roneparstat (SST0001), muparfostat (PI-88), and necuparanib (M-402), or obtained by synthesis as pixatimod (PG545). These HS mimics show various limitations, mainly related to their high molecular weight and heterogeneous nature, which could affect product characterization, standardization, and interpretation of biological data and are also related to their parenteral delivery route that might limit the patient compliance.(8) Moreover, despite expectancy from pre-clinical and early clinical trials, after interim analysis muparfostat was discontinued because it failed to meet the trial primary end point(17) and, similarly, the necuparanib trial was interrupted as a consequence of interim futility analysis.(18) However, the pixatimod trial was initially suspended due to local injection site reactions seen in patients, and the route of administration is currently being changed from subcutaneous to intravenous.(19,20) Recent clinical reports showed that pixatimod and roneparstat displayed good safety profile in advanced solid tumors and multiple myeloma in early clinical trials.(21,22)
    The development of small molecule inhibitors of Hpse has the potential to overcome some of the limitations of polysaccharides. Small molecules are, in fact, particularly desirable because they offer the opportunity to be properly designed to get favorable pharmacokinetic properties and oral availability. Among synthetic small molecules, some benzimidazole and benzoxazole derivatives have been described as Hpse inhibitors, such as 1-[4-(1H-benzoimidazol-2-yl)-phenyl]-3-[4-(1H-benzoimidazol-2-yl)-phenyl]-urea 1,(23) or benzoxazol-5-yl acetic acid 2,(24,25) that displayed good Hpse inhibitory potency (Figure 1).
    Very recently, our efforts to develop small molecule Hpse inhibitors led to the identification of 2-(4-(4-(bromo-methoxybenzamido)benzylamino)phenyl)benzimidazoles 3 (SST0867AA1) and 4 (SST0871AA1) as effective Hpse inhibitors, with IC50 values of 2.86 and 0.64 μM, respectively (Figure 1).(26) These compounds did not affect cell proliferation but were able to decrease the in vitro invasive ability of human cancer cell lines and to affect transcription of genes encoding for proangiogenic factors, effects that can be referred to their good ability to inhibit the Hpse enzymatic activity. Notably, the potency of these inhibitors against Hpse was enhanced by the presence of (i) fluorine atoms on the inner phenyl ring and (ii) amino acid residue on the lateral chain.
    Taking into account the good Hpse inhibitory activity of symmetrical inhibitors like compound 1(23) and suggestions provided by our previous molecular docking studies performed with compounds 3 and 4, we decided to design new symmetrical derivatives, introducing a more-rigid central portion in the structure to allow a better fit in the enzyme pocket, thus giving the opportunity for the lateral chains to have further positive interactions with the target.
    Thus, we designed and synthesized the derivatives reported in Chart 1. In detail, the new compounds are characterized by a rigid central core, typically a ureidic group, and by a symmetrical shape. The new derivatives show one or more of the following structural characteristics: (i) a benzoxazol-5-yl or benzimidazol-5-yl acetic acid scaffold; (ii) functionalization of the acetic acid moiety with amino acidic groups or aminoalkyl boronic acids; (iii) replacement of the benzoxazol-5-yl acetic acid scaffold with a 5,6-dimethylbenzoxazolyl one; (iv) replacement of the urea with a thiourea, a guanidine, an isopropanol, or acetone; (v) introduction of a 1,3-di(piperidin-4-yl)urea replacing the 1,3-diphenylurea; (vi) benzazole ring opening and introduction of an ortho-hydroxyamide, amide or sulfonamide function; (vii) replacement of the 2-(3-benzamidophenyl)acetic acid with a 3-benzamidobenzoic acid or (3-benzamidophenyl)boronic acid; (viii) shift of the urea moiety from para to meta position of the inner rings; and (ix) introduction of a fluorine atom in ortho to urea or thiourea moiety.
    The newly synthesized compounds have been evaluated in vitro for their capacity to inhibit Hpse enzymatic activity and to affect the proliferation and invasive potential of malignant cells. In addition, the effect of selected compounds on the expression of genes encoding for proangiogenic factors has been assessed in a tumor cell line. Moreover, based on docking studies in the crystal structure of human Hpse, a rationalization of the interaction between the best-acting compounds and the biological target has been proposed.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The benzazole derivatives 5a–e, 6a,b, 7a–c,g, and 8a,b were synthesized as reported in Scheme 1. Urea and thiourea derivatives 16a–d and 17a were obtained from the corresponding amines 12a–c(27) and 13(26) after reaction with triphosgene or thiocarbonyldiimidazole (thio-CDI) as a condensing agent. Differently, thioureas 16e and 17b have been obtained by microwave-assisted reaction of the amines 12c and 13 with isothiocyanates 14 and 15, which have been generated by reaction of the same amines, 12c and 13, with thiophosgene.
    Next, the esters 16a–e and 17a,b were hydrolyzed in alkaline medium (LiOH or NaOH) to give the corresponding acid derivatives 5a–e and 6a,b. The latter underwent coupling reactions with the proper amino ester in the presence of 1-ethyl-3-(3-dimethylaminopropyl)carbodiimide hydrochloride (EDCI) and 4-dimethylaminopyridine (DMAP) using N,N-diisopropylethylamine (DIPEA) as base to give esters 18a–c,g and 19a,b that were, in turn, hydrolyzed in alkaline medium to give the corresponding amino acid derivatives 7a–c,g and 8a,b.
    Guanidine derivative 5f was synthesized according to Scheme 2. Compound 16d was reacted with hexamethyldisilazane in the presence of EDCI to furnish guanidine 20 that subsequently underwent to alkaline hydrolysis with LiOH to give the corresponding acid derivative 5f.
    Boronic acids 7e,f and 8c and isopentyl derivative 7d were synthesized as described in Scheme 3. The appropriate alkyl boronic ester as ammonium salts were coupled with benzazol-5-yl acetic acid derivatives 5c and 6a in the presence of 2-(1H-benzotriazole-1-yl)-1,1,3,3-tetramethylaminium tetrafluoroborate (TBTU) and DIPEA at −80 °C for 3.5 h, giving the pinanediol boronic ester derivative 21 as previously described in the literature(28) or boronic acids 7f and 8c.
    We speculated that pinanediol esters are more resistant to hydrolysis(29) and, therefore, an oxidative cleavage of the pinanediol was carried out by using sodium metaperiodate in the presence of NH4OAcaq 0.1 N, according to a known procedure reported for boronic esters.(30) The use of such electrophilic oxidant was due to the awareness of the susceptibility of boronic acids to nucleophilic oxidative agents.
    Interestingly, by modifying both coupling-reaction conditions and coupling agent, the isopentylacetamide derivative 7d was obtained. In fact, this compound was obtained from the benzoxazol-5-yl acetic acid 5c that was reacted with (R)-boroLeu-(+)-pinanediol HCl in the presence of EDCI and DMAP, using DIPEA as a base, at room temperature for 24 h.
    5,6-Dimethylbenzoxazolyl derivatives 9a and 9b were synthesized as shown in Scheme 4. 2-Amino-4,5-dimethylphenol(31) was acylated with the proper acyl chloride in the presence of triethylamine to furnish amides 22a and 22b that subsequently underwent to ring closure using p-toluenesulfonic acid (PTSA) as a catalyst, obtaining 5,6-dimethylbenzoxazolyl nitro derivatives 23a and 23b. After catalytic reduction, the amines 24a and 24b were reacted with triphosgene to give the corresponding urea derivatives 9a and 9b.
    Alicyclic urea derivatives 10a and 10b were achieved as depicted in Scheme 5. 2-Amino-4,5-dimethylphenol(31) or methyl 2-(3-amino-4-hydroxyphenyl)acetate(27) were reacted with potassium O-ethyl xanthogenate that can act as a double electrophilic reagent, thus providing a convenient synthetic equivalent of thiophosgene.(32) The resulting unstable 2-mercaptobenzoxazole intermediates immediately underwent to nucleophilic substitution by treating the sulfur moiety with 4-(N-Boc-amino)piperidine,(33) resulting in derivatives 25a and 25b. Following deprotection of the Boc-protected amine by means of trifluoroacetic acid, amine derivatives 26a and 26b were obtained and then condensed with triphosgene furnishing ureas 27a and 10b. Finally, the acetic acid derivative 10a was obtained through alkaline hydrolysis with LiOH of the corresponding ester 27a.
    Syntheses of amide derivatives 11a–h were performed as reported in Schemes 6 and 7. More in detail, compounds 11b,d–h were synthesized by applying a divergent synthetic approach, first building the central diphenyl urea, thiourea, isopropanol or acetone moiety, while derivatives 11a and 11c were obtained by synthesizing the amide function first.
    In particular, derivatives 11b,d-h have been synthesized as shown in Scheme 6. The key intermediates for these syntheses are the acids 33b,d,e,g.
    The (thio)ureas 33b,d, were obtained by alkaline hydrolysis of the corresponding esters 29b,d. The urea 29b was synthesized by the reaction of methyl 4-aminobenzoate with triphosgene using triethylamine as a base, while the thiourea 29d was obtained via the microwave-assisted reaction of methyl 4-amino-3-fluorobenzoate with 28. This isothiocyanate has been achieved by reaction of the same methyl 4-amino-3-fluorobenzoate with thiophosgene.
    The isopropanol and ketal derivatives 33e,g were synthesized by reaction with aqueous base of the corresponding esters 32 and 31, respectively. Both compounds have been obtained starting from the joint intermediate 30, which reacted with ethylene glycol to furnish ketal 31, or underwent reduction by NaBH4 to give the corresponding alcohol 32. The key 1,3-diphenylacetone derivative 30 was obtained via a TosMIC (p-toluenesulfonylmethyl isocyanide)-modified reaction following a procedure previously reported in the literature.(34)
    Thus, acids 33b,d,e,g were then transformed into the corresponding amides 34b,d–h by reaction with the proper amine (methyl 2-(3-aminophenyl)acetate for 34b,d,f,g; methyl 2-(3-amino-4-hydroxyphenyl)acetate for 34e, ethyl 3-aminobenzoate for 34h) using EDCI and 1-hydroxybenzotriazole (HOBt) and N-methylmorpholine (NMM) as a base, or via acyl chloride.
    Finally, the ester function of 34b,d–h was hydrolyzed in alkaline medium to give the corresponding acid derivatives 11b,d–f,h and 35. Notably, ketal 35 was subsequently deprotected by reaction with trifluoroacetic acid to give the corresponding ketone 11g.
    Scheme 7 describes the synthetic route to obtain derivatives 11a,c. Commercially available methyl 2-(3-aminophenyl)acetate or methyl 2-(3-amino-4-hydroxyphenyl)acetate were acylated with p-nitrobenzoyl chloride or p-nitrobenzenesulfonyl chloride, respectively, to give amides 36a(27) and 36c. Nitro derivatives 36c and 37, obtained after protection via acetylation with acetic anhydride of 36a, underwent to Leuckart or catalytic reduction of nitro group, giving the corresponding amines 38a,c that were reacted with triphosgene to obtain urea compounds 34a,c. Finally, acid derivatives 11a,c were obtained after an alkaline hydrolysis with LiOH.
    The aryl-boronic acid derivative 11i was synthesized from the corresponding bromo derivative 39 via boronic ester, as depicted in Scheme 8. 3-Bromoaniline was acylated with p-nitrobenzoyl chloride to give amide 39 that underwent to a cross-coupling reaction with bis(pinacolate)diboron in the presence of a catalytic amount of PdCl2(PPh3)2, obtaining boronic ester derivative 40.(35) Followed a catalytic reduction of nitro group, giving the corresponding amine 41 that was reacted with triphosgene to obtain urea derivative 34i. Finally, the oxidative cleavage of the pinacol ester group furnished the corresponding boronic acid compound 11i.(30)

    Evaluation of Biochemical Activities

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    In Vitro Screening for Hpse Inhibitory Activity

    All compounds were tested for their ability to inhibit Hpse catalytic activity by using an in vitro assay, originally developed by Hammond and co-workers based on the Hpse-mediated cleavage of the synthetic heparin fragment, the pentasaccharide Fondaparinux (AGA*IA), which corresponds to the methyl glycoside of the antithrombin III (ATIII)-activating pentasaccharide sequence of heparin.(36)Table 1 shows the IC50 values generated from the dose–response curve for compounds 5a–f, 6a,b, 7a–g, 8a–c, 9a,b, 10a,b and 11a–i and for reference compounds 1–4 and Roneparstat (SST0001).

    a

    Dose causing 50% inhibition of Hpse enzymatic activity as determined from dose response curves (mean of duplicates; SD always <10%) repeated at least twice in separate experiments, stated in both micromole concentration and micrograms per milliliter.

    In general, derivatives of the series 5–8 proved to be the best inhibitors of Hpse among the newly synthesized derivatives, showing 13 compounds (out of 18 tested) active in the low-micromolar range and sometimes also in the nanomolar range. Conversely, compounds belonging to series 9–11 gave less-interesting results, with 4 compounds (out of 13 tested) active in the micromolar range.
    Compounds 5 and 6 are characterized by a symmetric urea or thiourea, a benzazole heterocyclic core, and an unfunctionalized acetic chain. These compounds were very active, showing IC50s ranging from 0.18 and 6.02 μM, and only one derivative was inactive (5b, IC50 > 10 μM). A comparison between 5a (IC50 = 3.20 μM) and its meta isomer 5b leads to conclude that the orientation given by the meta derivative is fully detrimental for the enzyme inhibition.
    Replacement of the urea moiety of 5a by a guanidine group gave compound 5f that showed a 2-fold decrease of the inhibitory potency (5a, IC50 = 3.20 μM; 5f, IC50 = 6.02 μM). Conversely, the substitution of the urea portion of 5a with a thiourea one led to compound 5d that resulted nearly two times more active than the parent compound (5a, IC50 = 3.20 μM; 5d, IC50 = 1.81 μM). Thus, it is possible to state that for non fluorinated benzoxazoles 5a,d,f the inhibitory activity decreases in the following order: thiourea > urea > guanidine.
    The same approach was applied to fluorinated benzoxazoles 5c and 5e. However, in this case, the urea derivative was found about 10 times more active than the thiourea counterpart (5c, IC50 = 0.18 μM; 5e, IC50 = 1.40 μM). Finally, within the small series of benzimidazole compounds, a comparable potency was observed between urea derivative 6a and its thiourea counterpart 6b (6a, IC50 = 0.98 μM; 6b, IC50 = 0.99 μM).
    Interestingly, the thiourea benzimidazole derivative 6b (IC50 = 0.99 μM) and its benzoxazole analogue 5e (IC50 = 1.40 μM) showed comparable inhibitory potencies, suggesting that both scaffolds are effective in inhibiting the targeted enzyme, in a similar fashion to our previous findings.(26) However, the urea 5c showed a 5-fold increase of potency if compared to its benzimidazole counterpart 6a (5c, IC50 = 0.18 μM; 6a, IC50 = 0.98 μM), suggesting that the presence of the fluorinated benzoxazole scaffold along with urea moiety may cause a favorable cooperative effect on the enzymatic inhibition.
    As we previously observed,(26) the introduction of a fluorine atom on the inner ring, in the ortho position to the urea group, gave a strong increase of anti-Hpse activity. This observation was confirmed in this work, in which we found that for example the fluorine derivative 5c was almost 20 times more active than its non fluorinated analogue 5a (5a, IC50 = 3.20 μM; 5c, IC50 = 0.18 μM). The same trend, although less relevant, was retained for the thiourea compounds. In fact, the fluorinated compound 5e showed IC50 = 1.40 μM, while its unfluorinated counterpart 5d showed IC50 = 1.81 μM.
    In another series of derivatives, the benzoxazol-5-yl acetic acid scaffold of compounds 5a and 5c was replaced with a 5,6-dimethylbenzoxazolyl one, resulting in derivatives 9a and 9b. In both cases, this structural modification did not improve the inhibitory potency, leading to inactive compounds and highlighting that the introduction of a fluorine atom in ortho position of the urea moiety of 9a did not positively affect the activity. Notably, 5,6-dimethylbenzoxazolyl derivative 9a proved to be also less effective than its parent compound 1 characterized by a 5,6-dimethylbenzimidazolyl scaffold, allowing us to hypothesize that the 5,6-dimethylbenzoxazolyl core does not represent a useful strategy for Hpse inhibition.
    To better understand the role of the 1,3-diphenylurea moiety in inhibiting Hpse, we designed 1,3-di(piperidin-4-yl)ureas, obtaining acetic and dimethyl derivatives 10a and 10b, respectively. This replacement led to compound completely inactive, proving the very important role played by the 1,3-diphenyl urea core to design an effective Hpse inhibitor.
    A further aspect that we decided to examine in depth was the effect on the anti-Hpse activity of compounds endowed with more extended shape if compared with the acetic derivatives 5 and 6. In fact, we recently proved that molecules characterized by the elongated shape and side-chain endowed with increased degrees of freedom could have a good fit with the long substrate binding pocket of Hpse.(26) Thus, we designed and synthesized derivatives 7 and 8 as amino acid derivatives of 5 and 6, respectively.
    In particular, the functionalization of the acetic acid portion of 5c with various amino acids led to derivatives 7a–c, which proved to be active, although less potent than the parent compound. Indeed, glycine (7a, IC50 = 0.45 μM), alanine (7b, IC50 = 0.37 μM) and phenylalanine (7c, IC50 = 0.72 μM) derivatives, even though reported submicromolar inhibitory potency, were 2–4 times less active than the corresponding acetic acid counterpart 5c. Thus, in this subseries the activity decreases with the following order: Ala > Gly > Phe.
    Very interestingly, the introduction of the glycine lateral chain on the thiourea 5e, gave the corresponding amide 7g that showed a 18-fold increase in potency in respect to its parent compound 5e (7g, IC50 = 0.08 μM; 5e, IC50 = 1.40 μM). Moreover, the thiourea 7g was also 6 times more active than its urea counterpart 7a (IC50 = 0.45 μM).
    Derivative 7g was the most-potent inhibitor among the newly synthesized anti-Hpse compounds, showing nanomolar potency. It could be interesting to compare the activity of the small molecule 7g to that of polysaccharide derivative SST0001, one of the most potent Hpse inhibitor reported (that is, in clinical trials). In fact, although 7g is 16 times less potent that SST0001 if the activity is expressed in molar concentration (7g, IC50 = 0.08 μM; SST0001, IC50 = 0.005 μM), the amount potentially necessary for its clinical use could be about the half of that of SST0001 due to their huge difference in molecular weight (7g, IC50 = 0.06 μg/mL; SST0001, IC50 = 0.10 μg/mL).
    The effect of amino acid introduction was also investigated within the benzimidazole series by functionalizing the acetic acid portion of 6a with glycine or phenylalanine residues to obtain 8a and 8b, respectively. Surprisingly, the glycine derivative 8a proved to be inactive, while the phenylalanine 8b showed IC50 = 0.82 μM, comparable to that of the parent compound 6a (IC50 = 0.98 μM).
    We also investigated the effect of the acetic acid functionalization of benzoxazole compound 5c by introducing an isopentylamine group to obtain the amide 7d, resulting in a complete loss of activity, thus confirming our previous findings demonstrating the relevance of hydrophilic groups in establishing polar interactions with the enzyme.(26)
    Notably, the activity of groups that could mimic the glycine moiety was evaluated by its substitution with an (aminomethyl)boronic acid group. Indeed, derivatives 7f and 8c represent the boronic acid counterparts of benzoxazole 7a and of benzimidazole 8a, respectively. Both boronic acid derivatives showed inactivity at the tested concentrations, suggesting that such substitution is detrimental for the inhibition. The combination between an alkyl group (an isopentyl substituent) and boronic acid gave compound 7e endowed with marginal activity (IC50 = 4.87 μM) if compared with the activities of the remaining amino acid derivatives. However, 7e proved to be better if compared with its parent compounds 7d and 7f both resulting inactive (IC50 > 10 μM). Finally, we decided to open the benzoxazole ring and/or to modify the central urea moiety obtaining derivatives of series 11 with the aim of modifying the planar shape of benzazole derivatives.
    The compound characterized by the presence of an ortho-hydroxyphenyl amide group (11a) showed comparable activity to the corresponding benzoxazole derivative 5a (11a, IC50 = 2.73 μM; 5a, IC50 = 3.20 μM), suggesting that the ring opening does not affect the inhibitory potency. As a matter of fact, by opening the benzoxazole ring and replacing the hydroxy group with a hydrogen (11b), a slight gain of activity was reached (IC50 = 1.76 μM). Indeed, compound 11b proved to be 1.5 times more potent than its hydroxylated analogue 11a and nearly 2 times more active than its benzoxazole counterpart 5a. On the contrary, the replacement of the amide group of 11b with a sulfonamide one (11c) led to an activity loss. The same approach of ring opening was applied for thiourea benzoxazole derivative 5e, thus obtaining thiourea amide compound 11d. Differently from the trend observed with urea amide derivative 11b; in this case, the opening of benzoxazole ring led to a 2-fold activity decrease (11d, IC50 = 2.61 μM; 5e, IC50 = 1.40 μM), suggesting that for thiourea derivatives this approach is not useful, in contrast to what previously noticed for both acetic acid and glycine benzoxazole compounds. In the same way, the replacement of the central core of the structure (the urea group) with an acetonyl or 2-hydroxypropyl moiety led to activity loss. More in detail, the replacement of the urea portion of 11a and 11b with a 2-hydroxypropyl group gave 11e and 11f, respectively, which were inactive at the tested concentration, suggesting that the urea moiety is important for anti-Hpse activity. Accordingly, the same trend was observed by substituting the urea of 11b with a 2-propanonyl moiety (11g). Indeed, the inactivity of compound 11g confirmed the aforementioned hypothesis according to which the urea moiety is relevant for Hpse inhibition.
    Also for amide derivatives 11, structural modifications of the acetic acid group were carried out. Indeed, the acetic acid moiety of derivative 11b was replaced with a carboxylic acid group and boronic acid function (11h and 11i, respectively). Both compounds 11h and 11i resulted inactive as Hpse inhibitors in biochemical assay, thus denoting that acetic acid group can positively contribute to the enzymatic inhibitory activity.

    Molecular Modeling

    To investigate the putative binding mode of symmetrical Hpse inhibitors and rationalize the role of the terminal acidic groups of the most-potent inhibitors, molecular docking studies were performed for the reference compound 1 and the benzoxazole derivatives 5c and 7g into the crystal structure of human Hpse. Mature human Hpse is a heterodimer composed of the N-terminal 8 kDa and C-terminal 50 kDa chains noncovalently assembled into a (β/α)8-TIM barrel fold. As revealed by the X-ray structures of the enzyme in complex with saccharide derivatives,(37) the active site of Hpse is characterized by a narrow, extended channel with the catalytic residues E225 and E343 placed in the middle of the substrate binding site. At the two extremities of the channel, the binding site is flanked by the heparin binding domain 1 (HBD-1, residues 158–171) and heparin binding domain 2 (HBD-2, residues 270–280), identified as protein motifs involved in the interaction with the heparan sulfate substrate.(38) Docking studies were performed into a model of GS3 Hpse (that is, a catalytically active form of the enzyme in which the 8 and 50 kDa chains are linked together by a short peptide)(39) because this enzyme construct was used for the evaluation of the inhibitory activity of the newly synthesized compounds. GS3 model building starting from the X-ray structure 5E9C has already been described.(26) Analysis of docking poses reveals that compounds 1, 5c, and 7g assume similar binding modes. They were accommodated within the substrate binding site of the enzyme and placed the urea or thiourea group close to the catalytic residue E225 (Figure 2), overlapping the position occupied by the cocrystallized tetrasaccharide inhibitor dp4 (Figure 2A). Although the three compounds are accommodated within the substrate binding site with similar geometries, inhibitors 5c and 7g interact with HBD-1 and HBD-2 regions, thus undertaking a higher number of polar contacts with the protein counterpart. The urea group of compound 1 occupies the catalytic site with its nitrogen atoms interacting with the side chain of E225. 5,6-Dimethylbenzimidazoles form hydrogen bonds with G350 and N227 (Figure 3A). G350 is a relevant amino acid for the recognition of Hpse substrates as its backbone NH interacts with the carboxylic group of the glucuronic acid portion accommodated into the catalytic site. The binding mode of 5c reveals that, similarly to compound 1, the urea group occupies the catalytic site, with the nitrogen atoms interacting with E225 and the carbonyl oxygen forming an additional hydrogen bond with K231, which lies on a short helix positioned between HBD-1 and HBD-2 (Figure 2B). The terminal carboxylic groups undertake polar intractions with residues N64, A388 and G389 on one side, and with R273 and the backbone of K274 belonging to HBD-2 on the opposite side. A cation-π interaction might be envisaged between R272 (HBD-2) and one of the phenylbenzoxazole portions. Amino acids interacting with the inhibitors are also involved in substrate recognition. In fact, in crystal structures the backbone NH of G389 is hydrogen-bonded to a sulfate group of a glucosamine, while that of A388 interacts with the same sulfate by the interposition of a water molecule. A similar binding pose was obtained also for the thiourea derivative 7g, with the conjugated amino acids interacting with HBD-2 (residues N227, R272, and R273), and with N64, K98, and G350 on the opposite site (Figure 2C). Being both the HBD-1 and HBD-2 regions highly polar and rich in basic amino acids, the terminal acidic portions of compounds 5c and 7g can efficiently interact with the surrounding protein residues, which might explain the higher inhibitory potency observed for these compounds compared to reference 1.
    However, docking studies were not able to catch all finer structure–activity relationships (SAR), probably because compounds were docked into the crystal structure of heparanase in complex with a sulfated glycoside inhibitor, and large conformational arrangements may play a role in the mutual adaptation of inhibitor and binding site. Thus, the differential role of fluorination of the benzene ring in the benzoxazole and benzimidazole series could not be accounted for by docking scores calculated for compounds 5c, 5e, 6a, and 6b. However, the interaction energy estimated by the docking procedure (Emodel; see Table S1), correctly predicted the rank order of potencies for nonfluorinated derivatives with various central linkers, i.e., thiourea (5d), urea (5a), and guanidine (5f). Indeed the three compounds interact with the catalytic residue E225 through their thiourea, urea, and guanidine NH groups, assuming arrangements similar to that depicted in Figure 2 for compound 5c. Values of the Emodel for the best docking solution of the three compounds (in order of decreasing potency) were −108.7 kcal/mol for 5d, −106.1 kcal/mol for 5a, and −93.4 kcal/mol for 5f.
    Molecular dynamics (MD) was applied to evaluate the stability of the binding poses identified by docking studies. The Hpse-inhibitor complexes were stable during short MD simulations (10 ns, corresponding to the 100 frames analyzed in Figure S1 for a representative simulation). In Figure S1, the root-mean-square deviation (RMSD) of compound 5c heavy atoms and the total number of hydrogen bonds undertaken by its carboxylate groups are reported. During the simulation the inhibitor remains within the substrate binding cleft, with the urea fragment anchored to E225. The increase of RMSD is due to the flexible terminal acetic groups, which interact with different amino acid counterparts during the simulation, yet the average number of hydrogen bonds is conserved throughout the simulation.

    Evaluation of Biological Activities

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Proliferation Assay

    A few compounds (5a, 5c, 7a–c, and 7g as well as reference compound 3)(26) found active in the enzyme assay were further characterized in vitro by a cell proliferation assay to assess their effect on the growth of three human tumor cell lines, namely HT1080 (fibrosarcoma), U2OS (osteosarcoma), and U87MG (glioma) expressing different levels of Hpse.(40−42) Cells were treated for 72 h with serial dilutions (in the range 10–0.2 μM, covering the active concentrations in the Hpse enzyme assay) of each test compound as well as of the internal reference compound 3. As determined by cell proliferation curves (not shown), none of the newly synthesized derivatives showed antiproliferative activity up to the maximum concentration assessed (10 μM), whereas the internal reference compound 3 moderately inhibited proliferation of the three cell lines (IC50 values between 1.7 and 8.7 μM) (Table 2). Overall, these data show that the newly synthesized compounds are able to inhibit Hpse at concentrations not interfering with tumor cell proliferation, making their use particularly advantageous for therapeutic applications in non-oncology fields.

    Starting from these data achieved on tumor cell lines expressing moderate amount of Hpse protein, we wondered if the result would have been the same against tumor cells expressing highest levels of Hpse. To this end, we have chosen to test the putative antiproliferative activity of compound 5c, against the human CME-1 synovial sarcoma cell line, known to express high levels of Hpse.(43)
    As shown in Figure 3A, compound 5c inhibited CME-1 cell proliferation in a dose-dependent way, with IC50 = 9.5 ± 1.0 μM.

    Invasion Assay

    According to the well-known pivotal role of Hpse in promoting cancer cell invasion and metastasis, we examined the effects of selected representative compounds (5c, 7a, 7b, and 7g) on the invasive activity of human cancer cell lines HT1080, U87MG, and U2OS, by Matrigel invasion assay. All of the compounds were assayed at the fixed concentration of 10 μM that allowed the inhibition of Hpse enzymatic activity without affecting cell proliferation.
    Instead, the internal reference compound 3 was tested at concentration below the IC10 values measured in the proliferation assay against each cell line. Interestingly, as shown in Table 3, all the newly synthesized compounds were able to inhibit, although at a different extent, the invasion of the three tumor cell lines and, in most cases, with potency similar to that of the internal reference compound 3.
    Table 3. Inhibition of Invasive Potential of HT1080, U87MG, and U2OS Human Cell Lines by Derivatives 3, 5c, 7a, 7b, and 7g and Reference Compound 3a
    compoundconcentration tested (μM)HT1080U87MGU2OS
    5c10+++++
    7a10+++++
    7b10++++

    a

    Matrigel cell invasion assay on HT1080, U87MG, and U2OS tumor cells upon 24 h of treatment. Score symbols: plus sign indicates a <50% inhibition and double plus sign indicates a 50–90% of inhibition of cell invasion with respect to cell invasion in the absence of drugs.

    Because treatment with Hpse inhibitors heparin derivatives has been previously reported to heavily affect the invasive capacities of soft tissue sarcoma cell lines, including the CME-1 cell line,(43,44) we further investigated the anti-invasive activity of compound 5c in this cellular model. To this end, compound 5c was tested at three concentrations below the IC50 value measured by the proliferation assay on the same cell line.
    As shown in Figure 4, compound 5c inhibited Matrigel invasion by CME-1 cells in a dose-dependent way being active also at nontoxic concentrations (i.e., 1.55 and 3.12 μM), as resulted by the antiproliferative assay, thus resembling data seen on the other tumor cells reported in Table 3 (similar results were obtained with 7a and 7g on HT1080 cells; see Figure S2).
    The maximum effect was achieved at the highest dose, 6.25 μM, that is nearby the IC50 value measured after 72 h of drug treatment. It is noteworthy that in the Matrigel invasion assay, CME-1 cells were treated only for 24 h, and compound 5c was not able to affect cell proliferation after this time (data not shown). Altogether, these data would suggest that suppression of tumor invasion by these compounds might be mediated by inhibition of Hpse activity.

    Effects on Gene Expression

    We next wondered if our selected Hpse inhibitors are also able to affect transcription of genes, an effect associated with the nuclear-located active form of Hpse that contribute to drive an aggressive tumor phenotype expression.(45,46) To this end, HT1080 cells were treated for 24 h with derivatives 5c, 7a, and 7g as compared to the internal reference compound 3 at concentrations allowing the inhibition of Hpse enzymatic activity but not of tumor cell proliferation. Then, the mRNA levels of genes encoding for proangiogenic factors, such as FGF1/2, VEGF, and MMP-9, and for Hpse (HPSE-1) were measured by a quantitative real-time polymerase chain reaction (q-PCR) assay.
    Overall, results highlighted a weak or moderate inhibitory effect of all the compounds on transcription of these genes (Figure 5), although none derivative appeared more efficacious than the others. Interestingly, only derivatives 7a and 7g were shown to decrease (about 40% with respect to control cells) transcription of the gene encoding for the same Hpse. Furthermore, compound 7g was more effective also in inhibiting the other proangiogenic factors evaluated.

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    In this study, we reported the design, synthesis and biological evaluation of symmetrical 2-aminophenyl-benzazolyl-5-acetate derivatives as new Hpse inhibitors. Interestingly, we found that these symmetrical compounds were more effective in inhibiting the targeted enzyme with respect to our previously reported asymmetrical benzazole derivatives (i.e., 3 and 4).(26) Indeed, the thiourea glycine benzoxazole compound 7g (SST0872AA1), with an IC50 of 0.08 μM, represents the best acting Hpse inhibitor among the newly synthesized compounds reported in this study. The relevance of compound 7g was also confirmed by invasion and real-time PCR test.
    As we previously reported, the introduction of a fluorine atom in the ortho position of the urea group remarkably improved the inhibitory profile, as suggested by the increase in inhibitory potency of urea 5c or thiourea 5d in respect to their non fluorinated analogues urea 5a and thiourea 5e. Moreover, the introduction of thiourea as central core seems to be a useful strategy, as confirmed by the non fluorinated acetic acid derivative 5d (in comparison to its urea analogue, 5a) and the fluorinated glycine derivative 7g (in comparison to its urea counterpart, 7a). Accordingly to our previously results, also for symmetrical inhibitors, it is possible to denote the relevant role of flexible lateral chain such as the acetic acid group and/or acetic group functionalized with amino acids, suggesting that side chains endowed with increased degrees of freedom could better fit within the binding pocket of Hpse.
    The best active derivatives 5c and 7g were evaluated through molecular docking studies, in comparison to reference compound 1. The in silico studies confirmed that the acidic portions of the flexible side chain of compounds 5c and 7g can efficiently interact with relevant Hpse protein residues of the catalytic site, which might explain the higher inhibitory potency observed for these compounds compared to 5,6-dimethylbenzimidazole 1.
    Interestingly, our findings highlighted the ability of Hpse inhibitors to affect biological processes related to Hpse enzymatic activity, confirming both the anti-metastatic and the anti-angiogenic potential of small molecule Hpse inhibitors. Indeed, representative compounds 5a, 5c, 7a–c, and 7g were proven to inhibit Hpse without interfering with tumor cell proliferation, differently from our previously reported asymmetrical benzimidazole derivative 3, suggesting useful applications in nononcology fields. Moreover, invasion assay confirmed the anti-metastatic potential of compounds 5c, 7a, and 7b, with potency comparable to that of our previously reported compound 3. Notably, 5c inhibited invasion in CME-1 cells at nontoxic concentrations, in a similar fashion to heparin-like Hpse inhibitors. Consistent with their ability to inhibit Hpse, likely resulting also in degradation of the nuclear heparin sulfate that is known to affect the control of gene transcription, compounds 5c, 7a, and 7g are also able to inhibit the transcription of genes encoding for proangiogenic factors in tumor cells, although a mechanism of inhibition not depending on the enzymatic activity of HPSE cannot be excluded. Overall, these findings identify in the compound 7g one of the most-potent inhibitors of Hpse and provide useful insights for the future optimization and development of highly potent selective anti-Hpse agents.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemistry: General

    Melting points were determined on a Bobby Stuart Scientific SMP1 melting point apparatus and are uncorrected. Compounds purity were always >95% determined by high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC). HPLC analyses were carried out with a Shimadzu LC-10AD VP CTO-10AC VP. Column used was generally Discovery Bio Wide Pore C18 and C8 (10 cm × 4.6 mm, 3 μm). IR spectra were recorded on a PerkinElmer Spectrum-One spectrophotometer. 1H nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra were recorded at 400 MHz on a Bruker AC 400 Ultrashield 10 spectrophotometer (400 MHz). Dimethyl sulfoxide-d6 99.9% (CAS 2206-27-1), deuterochloroform 98.8% (CAS 865-49-6), and N,N-dimethylformamide-d7 (CAS 4472-41-7) of isotopic purity (Aldrich) were used. Mass spectra were recorded on a ThermoFinnigan LCQ Classic liquid chromatography–tandem mass spectrometry (LC–MS/MS) ion trap equipped with an electrospray ionization (ESI) source and a syringe pump. Samples (10–4–10–5 M in MeOH/H2O 90:10) were infused in the electrospray system at a flow rate of 5–10 μL min–1. When necessary, 50 μL of 10–2 M HCOOH or 10–2 M NH3 were added to the sample solution to promote the analyte ionization. Column chromatographies were performed on silica gel (Merck; 70–230 mesh). All compounds were routinely checked with thin-layer chromatography by using aluminum-baked silica gel plates (Fluka DC-Alufolien Kieselgel 60 F254). Developed plates were visualized by UV light. Solvents were reagent grade and, when necessary, were purified and dried by standard methods. Concentration of solutions after reactions and extractions involved the use of rotary evaporator (Büchi) operating at a reduced pressure (ca. 20 Torr). Organic solutions were dried over anhydrous sodium sulfate (Merck). All solvents were freshly distilled under nitrogen and stored over molecular sieves for at least 3 h prior to use. Analytical results agreed to within ±0.40% of the theoretical values.

    Microwave Irradiation Experiments

    Microwave reactions were conducted using a CEM Discover system unit (CEM. Corp., Matthews, NC). The machine consists of a continuous focused microwave-power delivery system with operator selectable power output from 0 to 300 W. The temperature of the contents of the vessel was monitored using a calibrated infrared temperature control mounted under the reaction vessel. All experiments were performed using a stirring option whereby the contents of the vessel are stirred by means of a rotating magnetic plate located below the floor of the microwave cavity and a Teflon-coated magnetic stir bar in the vessel.

    General Procedure A (GP-A) to Obtain Acid Derivatives (5a-c,f, 6a, 7a-c, 8a,b, 10a, 11a-f,h, 33b,g, and 35)

    A solution of LiOH (5 mmol) in distilled water (1 volume) was added to a suspension of the appropriate ester (1 mmol) in 5 volumes of THF or DMSO (for derivatives 5f, 11b, 11d, and 11h) and the reaction was stirred vigorously for the proper time. The organic phase was removed under vacuum and the resulting suspension was acidified with 1 N HCl (pH 4–5). The solid that formed was filtered and then washed with water and dried under IR lamp to afford pure acids. For each compound are reported the amount of ester, THF or DMSO, time of reaction, retention factor (Rf), yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results.

    General Procedure B (GP-B) to Obtain Thiourea and Alcohol Acid Derivatives (5d,e, 6b, 7g, and 33d,e)

    A mixture of NaOH 20% (5 mmol) and the appropriate ester (1.38 mmol) in THF (50 mL) and H2O (20 mL) was stirred at room temperature (or reflux for derivative 33e) for the proper time. The solvent was removed under vacuum, and the crude product was treated with 1 N HCl until pH 3–4. The solid that formed was filtered, washed with water, and dried under an IR lamp to give pure acids. For each compound, the amount of ester, THF, time of reaction, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure C (GP-C) to Obtain Amino Ester and Isopentyl Derivatives (7d, 18a–c,g, and 19a,b)

    Amino ester hydrochloride (2.4 mmol), EDCI (2.4 mmol), DMAP (2.4 mmol), and DIPEA (4.8 mmol) were added into a well-stirred mixture of acid derivative (1 mmol) in anhydrous THF or DMF. The reaction was stirred to room temperature overnight under argon atmosphere. The organic phase was removed under vacuum and the crude product was treated with water. The solid that formed was filtered, washed with water, and dried under an IR lamp to afford the pure amide derivative. For each compound, amount of amino ester hydrochloride, THF or DMF, Rf; yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure D (GP-D) to Obtain Boronic Acid Derivatives (7e and 11i)

    NH4OAcaq (43.5 mL, 0.1 N) and NaIO4 (0.97 g, 4.52 mmol) were added into a stirred solution of the pinacol boronate ester (1 mmol) in THF (15 mL) (acetone for 41, 8 mL). The mixture was stirred vigorously at room temperature for 48 h (24 h for 11i), then the solvent was removed under vacuum, and the resulting solid was collected by filtration, washed with water and dried under IR lamp. For derivative 7e, the dried precipitate was washed with dichloromethane and reacted again in the same conditions described above for 24 h. The THF was removed under vacuum and the resulting solid was filtered, washed with water, dried under IR lamp, then washed again with dichloromethane, affording pure boronic acid derivative. For each compound, the amount of boronate ester derivative, Rf, yield (%), recrystallization solvent, and IR and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure E (GP-E) to Obtain Boronic Derivatives (7f, 8c, and 21)

    The appropriate ammonium salt (2.5 mmol), carboxylic acid derivative (1 mmol), and TBTU (2.75 mmol) were solubilized in dry DMF or THF and DMF, and the mixture was cooled to −80 °C while stirring. Then a solution of i-Pr2NEt (7.25 mmol) in dry DMF (4 mL) was added dropwise in 2 h, maintaining the temperature at −80 °C. The mixture was further stirred for 1.5 h and then slowly heated to room temperature. The solvent was removed under vacuum and the crude product was treated with distilled water; the solid that formed was filtered, washed with water, and dried under an IR lamp to afford the final product. For each compound, the amount of acid and ammonium salt, volume of solvent, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure F (GP-F) to Obtain Urea Derivatives (9a,b, 10b, 16a–d, 17a, 27a, 29b, and 34a,c,i)

    Triphosgene (0.18 mmol) (or thiocarbonyldiimidazole, 1.03 mmol, for 16d) was added to a stirred solution of the appropriate amine (1 mmol) and Et3N (1.05 mmol) in dry CH2Cl2 (dry CH2Cl2/THF 1:1 for derivative 34c, dry THF for derivative 34i) in an ice-cooled flask. After 10 min, the ice bath was removed, and the mixture was stirred under nitrogen atmosphere at room temperature (40 °C for derivative 16d) for the appropriate time. The solvent was removed under vacuum, and the crude product was treated with distilled water; the resulting solid was filtered, washed with water, and dried under an IR lamp to afford the products. For each compound, the time of reaction, amount of amine, volume of solvent, chromatography eluent (if applicable), Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure G (GP-G) to Obtain Isothiocyanates (14, 15, and 28)

    A mixture of the proper aryl amine (1.66 mmol), thiophosgene (2.2 mmol) in chlorobenzene (3.5 mL), and DMF (0.013 mL) was prepared at room temperature and slowly heated to reflux. Heating at reflux and stirring were continued for the proper time. Then, the mixture was cooled and concentrated under reduced pressure to give the pure isothiocyanates. For each compound time of reaction, the amount of amine, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure H (GP-H) to Obtain Thiourea Derivatives (16e, 17b, and 29d)

    A mixture of the proper amine (3.33 mmol) and the corresponding isothiocyanate derivative (3.5 mmol) in dry DMSO (1 mL) was irradiated with microwave at 80 °C for 3 min (or 1 min for derivative 29d) under argon atmosphere. After cooling, water was added (5 mL), and the precipitate that formed was filtered. The solid was washed with water, dissolved in THF and precipitated with n-hexane (for 16e), washed with THF (for 17b) or washed with 1 N HCl, and used for the next step without purification. For each compound, the amount of amine, amount of isothiocyanate, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure I (GP-I) to Obtain Amide Derivatives (22a,b, 34b,d,e, 36a,(27) and 39)

    To a suspension of the proper amine (1 mmol) in THF dry or CH2Cl2 dry under argon was added triethylamine (2 mmol) followed by a solution of appropriate chloride (1 mmol) in dry THF or CH2Cl2 (20 mL) in ice-bath and stirred for 3 h (30 min for derivative 34d and overnight for derivative 34b) at room temperature. Upon reaction completion, checked by means of MS, the solution was reduced under pressure, and water and ethyl acetate were added (200 mL). The aqueous layer was further extracted with ethyl acetate (2 × 50 mL). The organic fractions were combined, dried (with Na2SO4), and concentrated to give the subtitle compound that was used directly in the next reaction without purification. For each compound, the amount of amine, THF or CH2Cl2, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure J (GP-J) to Obtain Nitrobenzoxazole Derivatives (23a,b)

    p-Toluenesulfonic acid monohydrate (2.1 mmol) was added into a stirred suspension of dimethyl amide derivative (1 mmol) in toluene. A Dean–Stark trap was fitted, and the reaction was refluxed overnight. The reaction was cooled to room temperature, and the volume of solvent was reduced. The mixture was diluted with ethyl acetate (200 mL) and washed with saturated aqueous sodium hydrogen carbonate solution (200 mL). The organic layer was separated and the aqueous layer was further extracted with ethyl acetate (2 × 100 mL). The organic fractions were combined and washed with water (200 mL) and saturated sodium chloride solution (200 mL), dried (with Na2SO4), and concentrated to give the subtitle compounds. For each compound, the amount of amide, toluene, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure K (GP-K) to Obtain Arylamines (24a,b, 38a, and 41)

    A total of 10% palladium on activated carbon was added into a stirred solution of the appropriate nitro compound (1 mmol) in ethyl acetate under argon atmosphere. The reaction vessel was purged with hydrogen (40 psi) and stirred for the appropriate time. The mixture was filtered through celite and washed with ethyl acetate (100 mL) and dried (with Na2SO4). The solvent was removed under reduced pressure to give the subtitle compounds. For each compound, the amounts of nitro compound, ethyl acetate, reaction time, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure L (GP-L) to Obtain Piperinidyl Benzoxazole Derivatives (25a,b)

    To a stirred solution of commercially available 2-amino-4,5-dimethylphenol(31) or methyl 2-(3-amino-4-hydroxyphenyl)acetate(27) (1 mmol) in pyridine (1.8 mL) was added potassium O-ethyl xanthogenate (1.4 mmol).(32) The mixture was refluxed for 2 h under inert atmosphere. The reaction was then cooled to room temperature, diluted with ethyl acetate (400 mL), and washed with 2 N HCl (180 mL) and with saturated aqueous sodium chloride solution (400 mL) 5 times. The organic layer was dried (Na2SO4) and concentrated to give the corresponding unstable 2-mercaptobenzoxazole intermediates that immediately underwent to reaction with 4-(N-Boc-amino)piperidine (2 mmol) in m-xylene (8.5 mL). The mixture was refluxed for 1.5 h under inert atmosphere and then was diluted with ethyl acetate (400 mL) and washed with water and saturated sodium chloride solution (200 mL), dried (Na2SO4) and concentrated. The raw was purified by column chromatography (SiO2) to give the subtitled compounds. For each compound, the amount of amino phenol, chromatography eluent, recrystallization solvent, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure M (GP-M) to Obtain 4-Aminopiperinidyl Benzoxazole Derivatives (26a,b)

    To a stirred solution of 4-(N-Boc-amino)piperidinyl benzazoles (1 mmol) in CH2Cl2 anhydrous (10 mL) trifluoroacetic acid was added dropwise (2 mmol), and the reaction was stirred at room temperature for 45 min. The solvent was evaporated under vacuum and the crude was used for the next step without further purification. For each compound, the amount of 4-(N-Boc-amino)piperidinyl derivative, Rf, yield (%), IR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    General Procedure N (GP-N) to Obtain Acyl Chlorides of Acids 33a,b,d,e

    To a well-stirred solution of the proper carboxylic acid (1 mmol) in THF dry (15 mL) oxalyl chloride (15 mmol) and 4 drops of DMF dry were added, and the reaction was stirred at room temperature under argon atmosphere overnight (or for 4 h for derivative 33b). The solvent was evaporated under vacuum, and the crude was immediately used for the next step without characterization and further purification.

    General Procedure O (GP-O) for Coupling Reaction Derivatives (34f,g,h)

    EDCI (2.2 mmol), HOBt (4.4 mmol), NMM (4.4 mmol), and a solution of methyl 2-(3-aminophenyl)acetate (2.2 mmol) in DMF dry (6 mL) were added to a well-stirred solution of acid derivative (1 mmol) in DMF dry (6 mL). The mixture was stirred at room temperature for 4 h under argon atmosphere. The crude was quenched with water and extracted with ethyl acetate (100 mL). The organic layer was washed with saturated sodium chloride solution (400 mL), dried (with Na2SO4), and concentrated to afford the pure amide derivatives. For each compound, the amount of amine, Rf, yield (%), melting point (°C), recrystallization solvent, IR, 1H NMR, and MS (ESI) results are reported.

    Synthesis of Dimethyl 4,4′-(2-oxopropane-1,3-diyl)dibenzoate (30)

    To a stirred solution of the commercially available methyl 4-(bromomethyl)benzoate (0.50 g, 2.2 mmol) in CH2Cl2 (15 mL) was added TosMIC (0.21 g, 1.1 mmol) and TBAI (0.097 g, 0.26 mmol), followed by the addition of a solution of NaOH 40% (8.55 mL), and the mixture was stirred at room temperature for 2 h and 30 min under argon atmosphere. A solution of 2 N HCl was then added (0.6 mL), and the crude was extracted with CH2Cl2 (200 mL). The organic layer was dried (Na2SO4) and concentrated under vacuum to furnish a yellow oil that subsequently underwent to hydrolysis in HCl 37% (0.6 mL) in CH2Cl2 (3.42 mL) at room temperature for 30 min. The mixture was diluted with CH2Cl2 (200 mL), washed with saturated sodium bicarbonate solution (200 mL), dried (Na2SO4), and concentrated under vacuum. The raw was purified by column chromatography (SiO2, 7:3 hexane/ethyl acetate as eluent) to give the subtitle compound as a white solid (0.23 g, 64%). Rf (hexane/ethyl acetate 7:3): 0.3; 156–157 °C; toluene; IR ν 1708 (C=O ester), 1692 (C=O ketone) cm–1; 1H NMR (DMSO d6, δ): 3.76 (s, 6H, CH3), 3.93 (s, 4H, CH2), 7.24 (d, 4H, J = 8 Hz, Ar), 7.81 (d, 4H, J = 8 Hz, Ar). MS: m/z (ESI) calcd for [C19H18O5]+, 326.12; found, 327.18.

    Synthesis of Dimethyl 4,4′-((1,3-Dioxolane-2,2-diyl)bis(methylene))dibenzoate (31)

    To a solution of 30 (0.5 g, 1.53 mmol) in toluene (5 mL) were added PTSA monohydrate (0.003 g, 0.016 mmol) and ethylene glycol (0.1 mL, 1.79 mmol), and the mixture was refluxed overnight. The solvent was removed under vacuum and then diluted with ethyl acetate (150 mL), washed with saturated sodium bicarbonate solution (100 mL), dried (Na2SO4), and concentrated. The crude was purified by column chromatography (SiO2, 7:3 hexane/ethyl acetate as eluent) to give the subtitle compound as a gray solid (0.71 g, 80%). Rf (hexane/ethyl acetate 7:3): 0.57; 142–143 °C; toluene; IR ν 1713 (C=O ester) cm–1; 1H NMR (CDCl3-d, δ): 3.01 (s, 4H, CH2 benzyl), 3.43 (s, 4H, CH2 ketal), 3.93 (s, 6H, CH3), 7.36 (d, 4H, J = 8 Hz, Ar), 7.97 (d, 4H, J = 8 Hz, Ar). MS: m/z (ESI) calcd for [C21H22O6]+, 370.14; found, 371.01.

    Synthesis of Dimethyl 4,4′-(2-Hydroxypropane-1,3-diyl)dibenzoate (32)

    To a solution of 30 (0.1 g, 0.31 mmol) in 1:1 MeOH/THF (10 mL) was added NaBH4 (0.056 g, 1.5 mmol) in an ice bath, and the mixture was stirred at room temperature overnight. The solvent was removed under vacuum and then water was added (70 mL), and the precipitate that formed was filtered to give the subtitle compound as a white solid (0.70 g, 70%).     Molecular Modeling

    Molecular modeling studies were performed with the Schrodinger 2015-4 software suite. Ligand molecules were built with Maestro 10.4(47) and prepared with LigPrep 3.6.(48) Docking studies were conducted with Glide 6.9(49) using the SP scoring function.
    The structure of Hpse GS3 construct was modeled from the crystal coordinates of human Hpse in complex with the tetrasaccharide inhibitor dp4 (PDB code: 5E9C(28)), as described in ref (26). The docking grid was built using Glide 6.9(50,51) and was centered on the position of the tetrasaccharide inhibitor dp4, setting the dimensions of bounding and enclosing boxes to 15 and 50 Å, respectively. The binding poses were ranked according to the Emodel scoring function. The best-ranked docking solution was merged into the Hpse structure, and the complex was minimized with the OPLS2005 force field(52) implemented in MacroModel 11.0 using the Polak–Ribiere conjugate gradient method to a convergence threshold of 0.05 kJ mol–1 Å–1. During the minimization, the ligands and residues within 8 Å from them were free to move, while the backbone of other residues was kept fixed.
    Molecular dynamics simulations were performed using Desmond 4.8.(53,54) The protein was parametrized by applying the ff14SB Amber Force Field,(55) and the ligands were parametrized by applying the General Amber Force Field (GAFF).(56) Partial atomic charges of inhibitors were computed by the Antechamber module(57) of AmberTools 14 at the AM1–BCC level. The complexes were solvated with a box of TIP3P water by placing the box boundaries 12 Å far from the complex atoms on each side. The positive charge of the system was neutralized by adding Cl– counter-ions. After a relaxation protocol was implemented to progressively release the positional restraints applied to the heavy atoms of the complexes, 10 ns of unrestrained MD simulations were performed using the Langevin(58) coupling scheme in the NPT ensemble and by setting the temperature to 300 K. Bond lengths to hydrogen atoms were constrained using M-SHAKE.(59) Short-range electrostatic interactions were cut off at 9 Å, whereas long-range electrostatic interactions were computed using the Smooth Particle Mesh Ewald method.(60) A RESPA integrator(61) was used with a time-step of 2 fs, and long-range electrostatics were computed every 6 fs. MD simulations were replicated three times for each complex obtaining similar results.

    Biological Methods

    In Vitro Screening for Hpse Activity

    To determine the activity of the Hpse inhibitors, a homogeneous assay based on the cleavage of the synthetic heparin oligosaccharide Fondaparinux (Arixtra; Aspen) has been used. The assay measures colorimetrically the appearance of the disaccharide product of Hpse-catalyzed fondaparinux cleavage using the tetrazolium salt WST-1. The assay was essentially performed as described(36) with minor modifications. The assay was carried out with 100 microliters per well of assay solution containing 40 mM sodium acetate buffer (pH 5.0), 100 μM Fondaparinux, 2.5 nM recombinant Hpse (GS3), and serial dilutions of test compounds (tested in triplicate). Plates were sealed with adhesive tape and incubated, in the dark, for 3 h at 37 °C, followed by developing with 1.69 mM WST-1 (Santa Cruz biotechnology), for 1 h at 60 °C. Then, the absorbance at 560 nm was measured through a microplate reader (Victor 3, PerkinElmer). The IC50 value for each compound, versus Hpse, was calculated by GraphPad software. Finally, the measurements were corrected by subtracting both the reagent background and the inner absorbance value of test compound.

    Cell Lines and Maintenance

    HT1080 (fibrosarcoma), U87MG (glioblastoma), and U2OS (osteosarcoma) human cell lines were purchased from the American Type Culture Collection (ATCC; Manassas, VA) and maintained according to the manufacturer’s recommendations. Briefly, HT1080 and U87MG were grown in modified Eagle medium (Thermo Fisher Scientific), while U2OS cells were grown in McCoy’s 5a medium (Thermo Fisher Scientific), all supplemented with 10% FCS (Thermo Fisher Scientific), 100 U/mL penicillin, and 100 μg/mL streptomycin (Thermo Fisher Scientific) and 2 mM l-Gglutamine (Thermo Fisher Scientific). The human synovial sarcoma cell line CME-1 was provided by M. Pierotti(62) and cultured in RPMI medium (Lonza) supplemented with 10% FBS. All cell lines were maintained at 37 °C with a humidified 5% CO2 atmosphere.

    Proliferation Assay

    HT1080, U87MG, and U2OS exponentially growing cells were seeded into 96-well plates and then, 24 h later, treated for 72 h with several dilutions of test compounds (in the range 10–0.2 μM). Inhibition of cell proliferation was measured by means of a classical sulforhodamine B (SRB) assay performed in triplicate. The drug concentrations able to inhibit cell proliferation by 50% (IC50) were ultimately calculated from dose–response curves by using the GraphPad Prism 5.02 software.
    Alternatively, exponentially growing CME-1 cells were seeded in 12 wells per plate and, 24 h later, treated with the drug dissolved in DMSO or the solvent alone (0.5% final concentration). The anti-proliferative activity was evaluated after 72 h of drug exposure by cell counting. IC50 values were calculated as above.

    Matrigel Invasion Assay

    Cells were pretreated with the indicated drug concentrations in complete medium for 24 h. Then, cells were harvested, resuspended in serum-free medium, and transferred (2.5 × 104 HT1080, U87MG, and U2OS; 1.2 × 105 CME-1 cells per filter, respectively) to the upper chamber of 24-well Transwell plates (Costar, Corning Inc., Corning, NY) previously coated with Growth Factor Reduced Matrigel (BD Biosciences, San Jose, CA). The same drug concentration used for cell pretreatment was added to both the upper and lower chambers. After 24 h of incubation, cells that invaded the Matrigel were stained with SRB and then counted through a microscope at 40×–100× magnification depending on cell density. Statistical analyses were performed by the Student’s two-tailed t test. P < 0.05 was considered significant.

    Real-Time qPCR Assay

    Total RNA was extracted from HT1080 cells, upon 24 h of treatment with test compounds and then retrotranscribed using the iScript Advanced cDNA Synthesis Kit for RT-qPCR (Bio-Rad Laboratories Inc., Hercules, CA) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Real-time quantitative PCR analysis was performed using SsoAdvanced Universal SYBR Green Supermix (Bio-Rad Laboratories Inc., Hercules, CA) and the following PrimePCR SYBR Green Assays: qHsaCID0015228 (HPSE-1), qHsaCED0002206 (FGF-1), qHsaCID0015510 (FGF-2), qHsaCID0011597 (MMP-9), and qHsaCED0043454 (VEGF-A) (Bio-Rad Laboratories Inc., Hercules, CA). The 7900HT Sequence Detection System instrument and software (Applied Biosystems) were used to quantify the relative expression of the target genes.

Several recent reports provide important new insights into the mechanisms by which HSPGs function during development.

      Interactions of signaling proteins, growth factors and other proteins with heparan sulfate: mechanisms and mysteries
      Billings, Paul C.; Pacifici, Maurizio
      Connective Tissue Research (2015), 56 (4), 272-280CODEN: CVTRBC; ISSN:0300-8207. (Informa Healthcare)
      Heparan sulfate (HS) is a component of cell surface and matrix-assocd. proteoglycans (HSPGs) that, collectively, play crucial roles in many physiol. processes including cell differentiation, organ morphogenesis and cancer. A key function of HS is to bind and interact with signaling proteins, growth factors, plasma proteins, immune-modulators and other factors. In doing so, the HS chains and HSPGs are able to regulate protein distribution, bio-availability and action on target cells and can also serve as cell surface co-receptors, facilitating ligand-receptor interactions. These proteins contain an HS/heparin-binding domain (HBD) that mediates their assocn. and contacts with HS. HBDs are highly diverse in sequence and predicted structure, contain clusters of basic amino acids (Lys and Arg) and possess an overall net pos. charge, most often within a consensus Cardin-Weintraub (CW) motif. Interestingly, other domains and residues are now known to influence protein-HS interactions, as well as interactions with other glycosaminoglycans, such as chondroitin sulfate. In this review, we provide a description and anal. of HBDs in proteins including amphiregulin, fibroblast growth factor family members, heparanase, sclerostin and hedgehog protein family members. We discuss HBD structural and functional features and important roles carried out by other protein domains, and also provide novel conformational insights into the diversity of CW motifs present in Sonic, Indian and Desert hedgehogs. Finally, we review progress in understanding the pathogenesis of a rare pediatric skeletal disorder, Hereditary Multiple Exostoses (HME), characterized by HS deficiency and cartilage tumor formation. Advances in understanding protein-HS interactions will have broad implications for basic biol. and translational medicine as well as for the development of HS-based therapeutics.

      7
      Mammalian heparanase: gene cloning, expression and function in tumor progression and metastasis
      Vlodavsky, Israel; Friedmann, Yael; Elkin, Michael; Aingorn, Helena; Atzmon, Ruth; Ishai-Michaeli, Rivka; Bitan, Menachem; Pappo, Orit; Peretz, Tuvia; Michal, Israel; Spector, Larissa; Pecker, Iris
      Nature Medicine (New York) (1999), 5 (7), 793-802CODEN: NAMEFI; ISSN:1078-8956. (Nature America)
      Heparan sulfate proteoglycans interact with many extracellular matrix constituents, growth factors and enzymes. Degrdn. of heparan sulfate by endoglycosidic heparanase cleavage affects a variety of biol. processes. The authors have purified a 50-kDa heparanase from human hepatoma and placenta, and now report cloning of the cDNA and gene encoding this enzyme. Expression of the cloned cDNA in insect and mammalian cells yielded 65-kDa and 50-kDa recombinant heparanase proteins. The 50-kDa enzyme represents an N-terminally processed enzyme, at least 100-fold more active than the 65-kDa form. The heparanase mRNA and protein are preferentially expressed in metastatic cell lines and specimens of human breast, colon and liver carcinomas. Low metastatic murine T-lymphoma and melanoma cells transfected with the heparanase cDNA acquired a highly metastatic phenotype in vivo, reflected by a massive liver and lung colonization. This represents the first cloned mammalian heparanase, to the knowledge, and provides direct evidence for its role in tumor metastasis. Cloning of the heparanase gene enables the development of specific mol. probes for early detection and treatment of cancer metastasis and autoimmune disorders.

      Heparanase: a rainbow pharmacological target associated to multiple pathologies including rare diseases
      Rivara, Silvia; Milazzo, Ferdinando M.; Giannini, Giuseppe
      Future Medicinal Chemistry (2016), 8 (6), 647-680CODEN: FMCUA7; ISSN:1756-8919. (Future Science Ltd.)
      A review. In recent years, heparanase has attracted considerable attention as a promising target for innovative pharmacol. applications. Heparanase is a multifaceted protein endowed with enzymic activity, as an endo-β-D-glucuronidase, and nonenzymic functions. It is responsible for the cleavage of heparan sulfate side chains of proteoglycans, resulting in structural alterations of the extracellular matrix. Heparanase appears to be involved in major human diseases, from the most studied tumors to chronic inflammation, diabetic nephropathy, bone osteolysis, thrombosis and atherosclerosis, in addn. to more recent investigation in various rare diseases. The present review provides an overview on heparanase, its biol. role, inhibitors and possible clin. applications, covering the latest findings in these areas.

      Significance of Heparanase in Cancer and Inflammation
      Vlodavsky, Israel; Beckhove, Phillip; Lerner, Immanuel; Pisano, Claudio; Meirovitz, Amichai; Ilan, Neta; Elkin, Michael
      Cancer Microenvironment (2012), 5 (2), 115-132CODEN: CMAICW; ISSN:1875-2284. (Springer)
      A review. Heparan sulfate proteoglycans (HSPGs) are primary components at the interface between virtually every eukaryotic cell and its extracellular matrix. HSPGs not only provide a storage depot for heparin-binding mols. in the cell microenvironment, but also decisively regulate their accessibility, function and mode of action. As such, they are intimately involved in modulating cell invasion and signaling loops that are crit. for tumor growth, inflammation and kidney function. In a series of studies performed since the cloning of the human heparanase gene, we and others have demonstrated that heparanase, the sole heparan sulfate degrading endoglycosidase, is causally involved in cancer progression, inflammation and diabetic nephropathy and hence is a valid target for drug development. Heparanase is causally involved in inflammation and accelerates colon tumorigenesis assocd. with inflammatory bowel disease. Notably, heparanase stimulates macrophage activation, while macrophages induce prodn. and activation of latent heparanase contributed by the colon epithelium, together generating a vicious cycle that powers colitis and the assocd. tumorigenesis. Heparanase also plays a decisive role in the pathogenesis of diabetic nephropathy, degrading heparan sulfate in the glomerular basement membrane and ultimately leading to proteinuria and kidney dysfunction. Notably, clin. relevant doses of ionizing radiation (IR) upregulate heparanase expression and thereby augment the metastatic potential of pancreatic carcinoma. Thus, combining radiotherapy with heparanase inhibition is an effective strategy to prevent tumor resistance and dissemination in IR-treated pancreatic cancer patients. Also, accumulating evidence indicate that peptides derived from human heparanase elicit a potent anti-tumor immune response, suggesting that heparanase represents a promising target antigen for immunotherapeutic approaches against a broad variety of tumors. Oligosaccharide-based compds. that inhibit heparanase enzymic activity were developed, aiming primarily at halting tumor growth, metastasis and angiogenesis. Some of these compds. are being evaluated in clin. trials, targeting both the tumor and tumor microenvironment.
      >> More from SciFinder ®

      Heparin, heparan sulfate and heparanase in inflammatory reactions
      Li, Jin-ping; Vlodavsky, Israel
      Thrombosis and Haemostasis (2009), 102 (5), 823-828CODEN: THHADQ; ISSN:0340-6245. (Schattauer GmbH)
      A review. Heparan sulfate (HS) proteoglycans at the cell surface and in the extracellular matrix of most animal tissues are essential in development and homeostasis, and are implicated in disease processes. Emerging evidence demonstrates the important roles of HS in inflammatory reactions, particularly in the regulation of leukocyte extravasation. Heparin, a classical anticoagulant, exhibits anti-inflammatory effects in animal models and in the clinic, presumably through interference with the functions of HS, as both polysaccharides share a high similarity in mol. structure. Apart of regulation during biosynthesis, the structures of HS and heparin are significantly modulated by heparanase, an endoglycosidase that is upregulated in a no. of inflammatory conditions. Exploring the physiol. roles of HS and heparin and the mode of heparanase action in modulating their functions during inflammation responses is of importance for future studies.

      Ramani, V. C.; Vlodavsky, I.; Ng, M.; Zhang, Y.; Barbieri, P.; Noseda, A.; Sanderson, R. D. Chemotherapy induces expression and release of heparanase leading to changes associated with an aggressive tumor phenotype. Matrix Biol. 2016, 55, 22– 34,  DOI: 10.1016/j.matbio.2016.03.006
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      14
      Chemotherapy induces expression and release of heparanase leading to changes associated with an aggressive tumor phenotype
      Ramani, Vishnu C.; Vlodavsky, Israel; Ng, Mary; Zhang, Yi; Barbieri, Paola; Noseda, Alessandro; Sanderson, Ralph D.
      Matrix Biology (2016), 55 (), 22-34CODEN: MTBOEC; ISSN:0945-053X. (Elsevier B.V.)
      High heparanase expression is assocd. with enhanced tumor growth, angiogenesis, and metastasis in many types of cancer. However, the mechanisms driving high heparanase expression are not fully understood. In the present study, we discovered that drugs used in the treatment of myeloma upregulate heparanase expression. Frontline anti-myeloma drugs, bortezomib and carfilzomib activate the nuclear factor-kappa B (NF-κB) pathway to trigger heparanase expression in tumor cells. Blocking the NF-κB pathway diminished this chemotherapy-induced upregulation of heparanase expression. Activated NF-κB signaling was also found to drive high heparanase expression in drug resistant myeloma cell lines. In addn. to enhancing heparanase expression, chemotherapy also caused release of heparanase by tumor cells into the conditioned medium. This sol. heparanase was taken up by macrophages and triggered an increase in TNF-α prodn. Heparanase is also taken up by tumor cells where it induced expression of HGF, VEGF and MMP-9 and activated ERK and Akt signaling pathways. These changes induced by heparanase are known to be assocd. with the promotion of an aggressive tumor phenotype. Importantly, the heparanase inhibitor Roneparstat diminished the uptake and the downstream effects of sol. heparanase. Together, these discoveries reveal a novel mechanism whereby chemotherapy upregulates heparanase, a known promoter of myeloma growth, and suggest that therapeutic targeting of heparanase during anti-cancer therapy may improve patient outcome.
